Judge the Work, Not the Background
Editor's introduction and reviewer's commendation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a scholar's background in literature and poetry does not preclude expertise in kalam, and the importance of judging work on its merits.

Core Idea
Expertise in poetry and theology can coexist; judge the work, not the background.

This passage from the introduction to Sharh al-Maqasid shows the editor, Dr. Abd al-Rahman Umayra, being praised by his teacher, who initially doubted his theological acumen due to his literary background. It reminds us that scholars can excel in multiple fields, and that assumptions can be misleading.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Pay attention to the reviewer’s narrative tone.
Notice the shift from suspicion to admiration.
2
Identify the reviewer’s initial assumption
Find where the reviewer states his expectation that Dr. Umayra would be more poet than theologian. Note the reasoning.
The reviewer thought literati avoid rational theories.
3
Reflect on the correction
See how the reviewer’s opinion changed after examining the edition. What qualities did he discover?
The editor was deep, patient, and well‑versed in references.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفعلاً أوصت اللجنة المذكورة بأن يكون كتاب المقاصد هو أول كتاب يطبع ، غير أنه شاء الله تعالى ونعمت مشيئته أن يسبق توصية هذه اللجنة. الدكتور عبد الرحمن عميرة الأستاذ بكلية أصول الدين - أسيوط - بتحقيق هذا الكتاب وإخراجه إلى حيز الوجود، فقام مشكوراً بهمة عالية، باذلاً من جهده روقته وماله وسهره بالسير فيما قصد على الرغم من كثرة مشاغله، وقد تفضل وعهد إليّ مراجعة هذا التحقيق، فقبلت شاكراً له وفاءه، وحسن ثقته بأستاذه، وقد كنت قبل الاطلاع على هذا التحقيق أظن، وبعض الظن غير حسن، أن الدكتور عبد الرحمن عميرة، أديب وشاعر أكثر منه متكلماً، لأن من عادة الأدباء والشعراء أن يعنوا بالخيال والحس والوجدان والشعور، وأن يبحثوا عن المحسنات البديعية والمجازات والكنايات والتشبيه ليكون لنثرهم وشعرهم وقع حميل في نفس السامع، أما النظريات العقلية التي تحتاج إلى فكر وتأمل. وأما الأدلة والاعتراض عليها منعاً ،ونقضاً، وأخذاً ورداً، فالأديب والشاعر بعيد عن كل ذلك غير أني خاب ظني، وظهر لي أنه ظن خاطىء، لأني حينما راجعت هذا التحقيق، اتضح لي أن المحقق متكلم أكثر منه شاعراً وأديباً، في فهم النظريات العقلية، وفي نظم الأدلة وفي فهم ما يحققه على الرغم من صعوبته، كما تبين لي أنه عميق في تفكيره يغوص البحار، ويطوي القفار، يستسهل الصعب، صابراً مجداً، ساهراً منقباً، مضطلعاً على المراجع الكثيرة، ليصل بذلك إلى ما لذلك كله أكبرت فيه هذا العمل الضخم الأشم، وحق لي أن أفخر ببنوته وتلمذته والإنسان بجبلته لا يحب أن يساويه أحد، أو يفوقه، غير الأب مع ابنه، والأستاذ مع تلامذته، فإنه يحب لهم أكثر ما يحب لنفسه، ولهذا يؤمله ويرجوه . يقول الشاعر : ▼ قالوا أبا الصقر من شيبان قلت لهم كلا لعمري منه شيبان فكم أب بابن علا شرفاً كما علت برسول الله عدنان
And indeed the aforementioned committee recommended that the book Al-Maqasid be the first book to be printed, but God Almighty willed, and blessed be His will, that this committee's recommendation be preceded by Dr. Abd al-Rahman Umayra, professor at the Faculty of Usul al-Din – Asyut, in editing this book and bringing it into existence. He undertook, may he be thanked, with high zeal, spending his effort, time, money and sleepless nights in proceeding with what he intended despite his many preoccupations. And he favored and entrusted me with the review of this edition, so I accepted gratefully for his loyalty and his good confidence in his teacher. And before seeing this edition, I thought – and some thought is evil – that Dr. Abd al-Rahman Umayra was a man of letters and a poet more than a theologian, because it is the habit of men of letters and poets to concern themselves with imagination, sensation, emotion, and feeling, and to seek rhetorical embellishments, metaphors, metonymies, and similes so that their prose and poetry have a pleasing effect on the soul of the listener. As for rational theories that require thought and contemplation, and as for proofs and objections to them – prevention, refutation, acceptance, and rejection – the man of letters and the poet are far from all that. But my suspicion was disappointed, and it appeared to me that it was a false suspicion, because when I reviewed this edition, it became clear to me that the editor is a theologian more than he is a poet and a man of letters, in understanding rational theories, in arranging proofs, and in comprehending what he edits despite its difficulty. And it also became clear to me that he is deep in his thought, diving into seas, traversing deserts, making easy the difficult, patient, diligent, vigilant, well-acquainted with many references, until he reaches thereby what... for all that I greatly admired this enormous, lofty work, and it is my right to be proud of his academic sonship and his being my student. And man by nature does not love that anyone equals or surpasses him, except a father with his son and a teacher with his students, for he loves for them more than he loves for himself, and therefore he hopes and expects from them. The poet says: They said, 'O father of the falcon, is he from Shayban?' I said to them, 'No, by my life, he is from Shayban. How many a father has been raised in honor by a son, just as 'Adnan was raised in honor by the Messenger of God.'
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that the committee recommended al-Maqasid as the first book to be printed, but God willed that Dr. Abd al-Rahman Umayra, a professor at the Faculty of Usul al-Din in Asyut, preceded that recommendation by undertaking the edition and publication of this book. He did so with high zeal, spending effort, time, money, and sleepless nights, despite his many occupations. The author (the reviewer) was entrusted with reviewing this edition. The reviewer then confesses that before examining the edition, he had a suspicion—and it was an ill suspicion—that Dr. Umayra was more of a literary man and poet than a mutakallim (speculative theologian). He reasoned that literati and poets focus on imagination, emotion, and rhetorical embellishments, whereas rational theories requiring thought and contemplation, and arguments with their objections and refutations, are far from the literary person. However, the reviewer was disappointed in his suspicion; it proved wrong. Upon reviewing the edition, it became clear that the editor is more of a theologian than a poet or man of letters, in his understanding of rational theories and arrangement of proofs, and in his comprehension of what he edits despite its difficulty. The reviewer also found him deep in thought, traversing seas and deserts, making easy the difficult, patient, diligent, vigilant, well-acquainted with many references. Therefore, the reviewer greatly admired this immense work and felt proud of his academic sonship and studenthood. The passage ends with a poetic quote praising the editor through a metaphor of a father being elevated by his son, likened to the Prophet's lineage.

Key Terms
الدكتور عبد الرحمن عميرة
تحقيق
كتاب المقاصد
متكلم
أديب وشاعر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A scholar cannot excel in both literature and theology; literary background hinders theological depth.
Correction: This passage shows Dr. Umayra's literary skills complement his theological expertise, and initial assumptions were wrong.

Checkpoint
What was the reviewer's initial opinion of Dr. Abd al-Rahman Umayra?
A) That he was a great theologian
B) That he was more of a literary man than a theologian
C) That he lacked qualifications
D) That he was not a graduate of al-Azhar

Key Takeaway
A scholar's background in literature does not prevent deep theological insight; judge by the work itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone’s theological insight because you assumed their background in literature or poetry made them less capable? What concrete step will you take this week to check such assumptions?
?
Think of a person you have misjudged based on their expertise in a different field. How can you actively seek to discover their real capabilities, as the reviewer did with Dr. Umayra?
?
The reviewer felt pride as a teacher in his student’s achievement. Can you genuinely rejoice in the success of those you have taught or mentored, or does some part of you feel threatened? What would it take to develop that sincere joy?

Ali ibn Abi Talib's Warning on the Quran's Createdness
Anecdote of Ali's response to question about Quran's createdness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the early Muslim community, as exemplified by Ali ibn Abi Talib, viewed theological speculation about the Quran's nature as potentially dangerous.

Core Idea
Ali foresaw that debating the Quran's createdness would lead to serious conflict.

This short anecdote from early Islamic history illustrates the caution with which the Companions approached certain theological questions. When a man asked Umar about whether the Quran is created or uncreated, Ali immediately recognized the potential for a great fitna (trial). His response shows that even among the earliest Muslims, such speculative theology was seen as a path to division.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the scenario. Notice the presence of Umar and the unnamed questioner.
Pay attention to Ali's exact words.
2
Identify Ali's reaction
Observe that Ali does not answer the question directly but instead warns about its consequences. He says 'this word will have a consequence' and states that he would execute the questioner if he had authority.
Consider why Ali would suggest execution.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why the author includes this story in a theological work. It shows that the createdness debate was recognized early as divisive, and that some Companions considered it dangerous enough to warrant extreme measures.
This foreshadows later theological conflicts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخرج نصر أيضاً عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال: كنا عند عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه. إذ جاءه رجل يسأله عن القرآن. أمخلوق هو أو غير مخلوق؟ . فقال علي رضي الله عنه : هذه كلمة وسيكون لها ثمرة، ولو وليت من الأمر ما وليت، ضربت عنقه .
And Nasr also narrated from Abu Huraira (may God be pleased with him) who said: We were with Umar ibn al-Khattab (may God be pleased with him) when a man came to him asking about the Quran: 'Is it created or uncreated?' So Ali (may God be pleased with him) said: 'This is a word that will have a consequence. If I were in charge of the matter as you are, I would cut off his neck.'
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents another historical anecdote showing early Islamic wariness about theological speculation. A man asked Umar whether the Quran is created (makhluq) or uncreated. Ali ibn Abi Talib immediately warned that this question would lead to serious trouble, and stated that if he had Umar's authority, he would execute the questioner. The author uses this to highlight that the issue of the createdness of the Quran was recognized early as potentially divisive.

Key Terms
خلق القرآن
أمخلوق أو غير مخلوق
ضربت عنقه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate about the Quran's createdness only started in the later Abbasid period under the influence of Greek philosophy.
Correction: This anecdote shows that the question was already raised in the time of the Companions (Umar and Ali), indicating early awareness of the issue.

Checkpoint
What was Ali ibn Abi Talib's reaction to the question about the createdness of the Quran?
A) He said the Quran is created
B) He said the Quran is uncreated
C) He predicted it would have serious consequences and would have executed the questioner
D) He declined to answer

Key Takeaway
Ali ibn Abi Talib's immediate recognition of the danger in theological speculation about the Quran shows that the early Muslims regarded such questions as potentially divisive and harmful.

Abu Hanifa curses Amr ibn Ubayd for opening the door to kalam
Abu Hanifa condemns Amr ibn Ubayd for initiating futile theological discussions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam Abu Hanifa explicitly condemned Amr ibn Ubayd for opening the door to theological disputation about matters irrelevant to practical faith.

Core Idea
Not all kalam is praiseworthy; Abu Hanifa cursed the one who started useless debates.

The legacy of theological discourse (kalam) has always been contested. Here, we encounter a striking statement from Imam Abu Hanifa, the great jurist of the school of opinion. He curses Amr ibn Ubayd, a Mu'tazili scholar, for opening the path for people to engage in theological discussions about things that do not concern them. This passage challenges any assumption that Abu Hanifa approved of all speculative theology.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu Hanifa's statement
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the strong language: لعن الله (may Allah curse).
This is a direct statement from a major Sunni imam.
2
Identify the accused and the accusation
Abu Hanifa curses Amr ibn Ubayd, a Mu'tazili figure, for opening the way to discourse about what does not concern people. Understand who these figures are briefly.
Amr ibn Ubayd was a student of Hasan al-Basri and a founder of Mu'tazilism.
3
Consider the implications for kalam
This shows that Abu Hanifa made a clear distinction between permissible theological reasoning (like in fiqh) and blameworthy speculative kalam that leads to useless disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما صاحب مدرسة الرأي الإمام أبو حنيفة رضي الله عنه وأرضاه فإنه يقول: لعن الله عمرو بن عبيد، فإنه فتح للناس الطريق إلى الكلام فيما لا يعنيهم من الكلام
As for the master of the school of opinion, Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him and grant him pleasure, he says: 'May Allah curse Amr ibn Ubayd, for he opened the path for people to discourse about what does not concern them.'
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage reports a statement from Imam Abu Hanifa concerning Amr ibn Ubayd, who was a Mu'tazili scholar. Abu Hanifa explicitly invokes a curse ('la'ana Allah) upon Amr ibn Ubayd, accusing him of opening the door for people to engage in theological discourse (kalam) about matters that do not concern them. This reflects Abu Hanifa's strong disapproval of speculative kalam that goes beyond practical religion.

Key Terms
عمرو بن عبيد
الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam Abu Hanifa approved of all forms of theological speculation because he used reason in jurisprudence.
Correction: This passage shows he explicitly curses someone for opening the door to irrelevant theological discourse, indicating his strong opposition to speculative kalam.

Checkpoint
Why did Abu Hanifa curse Amr ibn Ubayd?
A) For denying predestination
B) For opening the path to irrelevant theological discourse
C) For opposing the caliph
D) For neglecting prayer

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa cursed Amr ibn Ubayd for initiating theological debates about matters that do not concern people, showing that not all kalam is acceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu Hanifa cursed a man for starting debates about what does not concern people. How many minutes today have you spent debating or arguing about a religious matter that had no impact on your actions or character? Are you willing to give up one such argument this week?
?
The phrase 'what does not concern them' is a mirror. Look at your last three online discussions or conversations about Islam. Were they truly necessary for your faith or practice, or were they mere speculation? What will you do differently tomorrow?
?
You have just read that a great imam invoked a curse for a specific type of speech. Does your heart tremble at the thought of falling into something that incurs Allah's curse? If not, ask yourself why—and then make a sincere intention to only speak about what benefits your deen.

Al-Shafi'i's Condemnation of Kalam
Al-Shafi'i prescribes flogging and public humiliation for those who engage in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical opposition of Imam al-Shafi'i to kalam and the severe punishment he prescribed for its practitioners.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i saw kalam as a dangerous departure from the Quran and Sunnah.

This passage records a famous and severe judgment from Imam al-Shafi'i against those who engage in kalam (speculative theology). His ruling—flogging, parading, public humiliation—reveals the intensity of early Sunni opposition to theological reasoning. Understanding this helps situate the later development of kalam as a discipline that had to overcome such condemnation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the full literal translation to understand the direct meaning.
Notice the harsh tone and specific punishments.
2
Identify the three punishments
Al-Shafi'i lists three components of the punishment: beating with palm branches, parading on camels, and public proclamation. Identify each in the text.
3
Reflect on the reason for the punishment
The proclamation states the reason: 'This is the punishment of one who has abandoned the Book and the Sunnah and devoted himself to kalam.' Consider why abandoning primary sources for kalam was seen as so grave.
Think about the early Islamic context.
4
Consider historical context
Al-Shafi'i lived in the early 2nd/3rd century AH when kalam was emerging. Reflect on how this opposition shaped later theological debates.
This is a key moment in the history of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإمام الشافعي هذا يقول عن علم الكلام وعن أصحابه : حكمي في أهل الكلام أن يضربوا بالجريد ويحملوا على الإبل ويطاف بهم في العشائر والقبائل وينادى عليهم : هذا جزاء من ترك الكتاب والسنة، وأقبل على الكلام
Imam al-Shafi'i, this one, says about 'ilm al-kalam and its adherents: 'My judgment concerning the people of kalam is that they should be beaten with palm branches, mounted on camels, and paraded among the tribes and clans, and a proclamation should be made over them: This is the punishment of one who has abandoned the Book and the Sunnah and devoted himself to kalam.'
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage records a severe judgment from Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the practitioners of kalam. He prescribes physical beating with palm branches (jarid), parading them on camels among the tribes, and publicly announcing that this is the consequence of abandoning the Quran and Sunnah for kalam. The harshness indicates al-Shafi'i's view that kalam is a dangerous innovation that leads people away from the primary sources of Islam.

Key Terms
أهل الكلام
الكتاب والسنة
الجريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Shafi'i allowed theological debate or was neutral toward kalam.
Correction: This passage shows his extreme opposition: he prescribed beating, parading, and public humiliation for kalam practitioners, explicitly stating they abandoned the Quran and Sunnah.

Checkpoint
What did al-Shafi'i prescribe as the punishment for people of kalam?
A) Banishment
B) Beating with palm branches and parading
C) Execution
D) Fines

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Shafi'i's punishment for kalam practitioners—beating, parading, and public humiliation—underscores the deep early suspicion of speculative theology as a departure from the Quran and Sunnah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself spending more time in speculative religious debates than in actually reading and applying the Quran and Sunnah? What concrete step will you take this week to rebalance that?
?
Al-Shafi'i prescribed a humiliating public punishment for those who abandon the two sources for kalam. If Allah were to expose your heart's attachments today, would you find that you have truly prioritized the Book and Sunnah over theological speculation?
?
Imagine you are being paraded in public for devoting yourself to arguments that distance you from revelation. Would that prospect shake you? What change does that fear demand of you right now?

Imam Ahmad's Stance on Kalam
Imam Ahmad forbids kalam and allows guidance for seekers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal's prohibition of kalam and the exception he made for guiding sincere seekers.

Core Idea
Imam Ahmad forbade kalam but allowed guiding a sincere seeker.

In this passage, we encounter the position of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, a towering figure of hadith and sunna. His conversation with a student reveals both a firm prohibition of engaging in kalam and a nuanced allowance for responding to those sincerely seeking truth. We'll examine his reasoning and the limits he set.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the dialogue structure between Imam Ahmad and his student.
Pay attention to the student's concern about hearing false kalam.
2
Identify the ruling
Imam Ahmad gives a clear ruling: 'Do not sit with them and do not speak to any of them.' Also, he warns against becoming famous for kalam.
3
Understand the reasoning
Ahmad argues that if kalam were good, the Companions would have done it. He calls it bid'a. Consider his emphasis on following the Salaf.
4
Note the exception
The student persists, and Ahmad says: 'If a seeker of guidance comes to you, guide him.' This softens the absolute prohibition.
This exception is key to understanding Ahmad's nuanced position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا انتقلنا إلى الإمام أحمد الله عنه ، نجد إنساناً جديداً من مدرسة القرآن، أنفق عمره في جمع حديث الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وتدوينه، تحمل الكثير من قساوة السجن والتعذيب ليبطل ما يقوله المبطلون من علماء الكلام وغيرهم عن القرآن وصفات الله سبحانه وتعالى . يقول الإمام أحمد لأحد طلابه عندما سأله رأيه في هؤلاء أصحاب الكلام : لا تجالسهم ولا تكلم أحداً منهم وقال له : إني ربما رددت عليهم . قال : اتق الله ، ولا ينبغي أن تنصب نفسك، وتشتهر بالكلام . لو كان في هذا خير لتقدمنا فيه الصحابة، هذه كلها بدعة . قال الطالب: «إني لست أطلبهم ، ولا أدق أبوابهم، ولكني سمعتهم يتكلمون بالكلام ولا أحد يرد عليهم ولا أصبر حتى يرد عليهم». قال أحمد : «إن جاءك مسترشد فأرشده وكررها مراراً
Then when we move to Imam Ahmad, may Allah be pleased with him, we find a new person from the school of the Quran, who spent his life collecting and recording the hadith of the Messenger of Allah (SAW), endured much harshness of prison and torture in order to invalidate what the false speakers among the people of kalam and others said about the Quran and the attributes of Allah, the Exalted. Imam Ahmad says to one of his students when he asked his opinion about these people of kalam: 'Do not sit with them and do not speak to any of them.' The student said: 'I sometimes respond to them.' He said: 'Fear Allah, and it is not appropriate for you to put yourself forward and become famous for kalam. If there were any good in it, the Companions would have preceded us in it. All of this is bid'a.' The student said: 'I do not seek them out nor knock on their doors, but I hear them speaking kalam and no one refutes them, and I cannot bear it until I refute them.' Ahmad said: 'If a seeker of guidance comes to you, guide him,' and he repeated it several times.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal's stringent opposition to kalam. The author first praises Ahmad as a man of the Quran and hadith who suffered persecution to defend correct beliefs. Then it reports a conversation: a student asks Ahmad's view on the people of kalam. Ahmad strictly forbids sitting with them or speaking to them. When the student expresses that he sometimes refutes them, Ahmad advises fear of Allah and warns against becoming known for kalam, arguing that if there were any good in it, the Companions would have engaged in it, and he declares it all bid'a. The student then explains his motivation: he does not seek them out but feels compelled to respond when he hears falsehood. Ahmad modifies his instruction: if a seeker of guidance comes to you (i.e., someone sincerely asking), then guide him. This shows a nuanced position: avoid kalam but respond to sincere seekers.

Key Terms
أصحاب الكلام
بدعة
الصحابة
مسترشد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam Ahmad completely forbade any form of defending the faith through argumentation.
Correction: Imam Ahmad allowed responding to a sincere seeker of guidance, showing a nuanced exception.

Checkpoint
What exception did Imam Ahmad make to his prohibition of kalam?
A) None at all
B) If the person is a ruler
C) If a seeker of guidance comes, then guide him
D) If it is in the presence of scholars

Key Takeaway
Imam Ahmad forbade sitting with kalam practitioners but permitted guiding a sincere seeker of truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about religion, is your aim to display your knowledge or to sincerely help someone find the truth? Imam Ahmad warned against seeking fame through kalam. What would a close inspection of your heart reveal about your motives in debates?
?
You know that the Companions did not engage in kalam, yet you find yourself drawn to theological arguments. Are you willing to step back and prioritize following their path over winning arguments? What specific step can you take today to seek knowledge in a way they would approve of?
?
If a sincere seeker came to you with a question about Allah's attributes, would your response be humble and guided by the Quran and Sunnah, or would it be laced with pride? How can you prepare yourself to be a source of guidance only when it is sought, and not a seeker of debating opponents?

Life of Imam al-Taftazani
beginner
5 min
Dialogue with Bedouins about a Prophet's Sign
Relates a pre-Islamic dialogue about a prophetic sign.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how pre-Islamic dialogues used predictive signs to argue for the coming of a new prophet.

Core Idea
A prophet's sign can be a simple prediction of future events.

This passage recounts a conversation between skeptical Bedouins and a prophet. The Bedouins, who reject revelation and the afterlife, demand a sign. The prophet gives a practical answer: a future prophet will come from the region of Mecca and Yemen, and the youngest person present will live to see him. This story illustrates the method of argumentation used by those who awaited a new messenger. As you read, notice how the sign is not miraculous but historical and personal.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Pay attention to the dialogue structure and the response of the prophet.
The footnote marker (۱) may indicate a scribal note; focus on the main narrative.
2
Identify the sign given
The prophet points towards Mecca and Yemen and indicates that the youngest person present will meet the future prophet. What kind of sign is this?
It is a predictive sign based on geography and lifespan.
3
Understand the dialectical context
The passage explains that this dialogue occurred between Bedouins who deny revelation and resurrection and people of scripture who believe in resurrection and await a prophet. How does this setting affect the impact of the sign?
It shows that the sign is tailored to the audience's worldview.
4
Reflect on the methodology
This story is an example of 'jadl' (debate) used to convince skeptics. Consider why such a simple sign might be effective.
It is tangible and verifiable over time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقالوا : ويحك يا فلان فما آية ذلك ..؟ قال : نبي مبعوث من نحو هذه البلاد وأشار بيده إلى مكة واليمن. فقالوا : ومتى نراه . . ؟ قال : فنظر إليَّ وأنا أحدثهم سناً فقال : إن يستنفذ هذا الغلام يدركه (۱) . عمره فهذه صورة من الجدل والحوار الذي كان يجري بين هؤلاء الأعراب الذين لا يصدقون بوحي ولا يؤمنون برسول ويشككون في الحياة الآخرة وما فيها من ثواب وعقاب ويقولون : إن هي إلا حياتنا الدنيا نموت ونحيا وما يهلكنا إلا الدهر». وبين أصحاب كتاب ورسول يدينون بعقيدة البعث ويبشر كتابهم الذي بين أيديهم بنبي جديد .
They said: 'Woe to you, O so-and-so, what is the sign of that?' He said: 'A prophet sent from near this country,' and he pointed with his hand towards Mecca and Yemen. They said: 'When will we see him?' He said: 'Then he looked at me—I was the youngest of them—and said: "If this youth lives long, he will meet him (1)." His age.' This is an example of the debate and dialogue that used to take place between these Bedouins who do not believe in revelation, do not believe in a messenger, and doubt the afterlife and its reward and punishment, saying: 'It is only our worldly life; we die and live, and nothing destroys us but time.' And between the people of a book and a messenger who believe in the doctrine of resurrection and whose book they have with them proclaims a new prophet.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents a historical narrative illustrating a form of argumentation before Islam. The Bedouins, skeptical of revelation and the afterlife, ask for a sign of a future prophet. The response is practical: the prophet points to a direction (Mecca/Yemen) and indicates that the youngest person present will live to see that prophet. The passage then comments that this is an example of the dialogues that occurred between such disbelieving Bedouins and the followers of a previous scripture who believed in resurrection and awaited a new prophet. The purpose is to show the intellectual climate and the existence of debate about religious truths before Islam.

Key Terms
آية (sign)
جدل (debate)
أعراب (Bedouins)
بعث (resurrection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This story is presented as a miracle or direct proof of prophethood.
Correction: It is an example of dialectical argument (jadl) used to address skepticism, not a supernatural sign.

Checkpoint
According to the anecdote, what did the prophet indicate as the sign of his coming?
A) A star would appear.
B) The youngest of those present would live to see him.
C) The sky would split.
D) A voice from heaven would call.

Key Takeaway
Dialogue and debate about religious truths existed before Islam, and the sign of a prophet could be a historical prediction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you ever like those Bedouins, demanding a visible sign before you truly submit to God? What specific sign would it take for you to obey without hesitation today?
?
The Bedouins said, 'nothing destroys us but time.' Do you live as if this life is all there is? How would your morning change if you deeply believed in the resurrection?
?
The youth was told he would meet the prophet. You have already received news of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). Are you living with the same eager anticipation of meeting him in the afterlife? What will you say when you meet him?

Life of Imam al-Taftazani

The Jurists' Role in Hardship
The author describes jurists who provided societal guidance during hardship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author positions himself against deviant thought and highlights the jurists' foundational role in guiding society through hardship.

Core Idea
Jurists laid principles to sustain society when resources were scarce.

This passage opens by describing the author's own refutation of deviant ideas among figures like al-Jawziyya and al-Taftazani. It then pivots to the positive contributions of jurists during wars and famines, showing how they codified rules and provided an ideal model for the Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Note the author's first-person claim of refuting deviant thought, then the shift to discussing jurists.
The passage moves from critique to construction.
2
Identify the two main themes
The passage has two clear parts: the author's attack on degenerate thought, and the jurists' work in laying down principles. Notice how the transition is made.
The word 'وإذا كانت الحرب' signals the shift.
3
Reflect on the jurists' achievement
The jurists mentioned—al-'Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, Ibn Raf'a, Ibn Jama'a—are praised for establishing rules and presenting an ideal society. Ask yourself why this was important in times of scarcity.
Their work was practical, not just theoretical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجوزية (۱) ، وسعد الدين التفتازاني وغيرهم. قد حاولت فكرهم المنحط ، ونازلت معتقداتهم المسفة في الضلال وأصابت منها مقتلاً، وإذا كانت الحروب قد أشاعت الفوضى، وأنزلت بالناس المجاعة، وتحول الكثير من أفراد المجتمع إلى لصوص وقطاع طرق، فإن فقهاء هذا العصر، قد قعدوا القواعد، وأصلوا الأصول، وقدموا لمجتمعهم الصورة المثلى لما يجب أن يعيش عليه المجتمع الإسلامي، إذا قلت موارده، أو ضاقت عليه سبل الرزق، وهذا ما فعله العالم الجليل المعز بن عبد السلام (۲) ، وابن رفعة (۳) ، وابن جماعة، وغيرهم من الفقهاء الأعلام . وأيضاً فإن تتابع الحوادث في هذا العصر، وتقلبات الليالي، وصروف الأيام، جعل علماء المسلمين يرصدون هذه الحوادث بدقة، ويتتبعون جزئياتها، بعين لماحة وفكر متقد، لتتخذ ذلك الأجيال اللاحقة دروساً وعبراً، فظهر المؤرخون العمالقة، أمثال العماد ابن كثير (۱) في موسوعته التاريخية (البداية والنهاية) وابن حجر العسقلاني (٣) في كتابيه المحلقين (الدرر الكامنة في أعيان المائة الثامنة) وكتاب (أنباء الغمر) وابن خلدون (۳) (مفخرة العرب) في دائرة معارفه الكبرى المسماة (العبر) وديوان المبتدأ والخبر في تاريخ العرب والعجم والبربر وغير هذا كثير. ولو ذهبنا تعدد صنوف المعرفة التي أنتجتها عقول المفكرين في هذا العصر لأعيانا الحصر والعد، ويكفي أن نقول في النهاية، إن عصر التفتازاني العلمي هو عصر عمالقة الرجال، وأفذاذ المفكرين الأبطال.
Al-Jawziyya (1), and Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani and others. I have attacked their degenerate thought and contested their foolish beliefs in error and struck them mortally. And if wars have spread chaos and brought famine upon people, and many members of society have turned into thieves and highway robbers, then the jurists of this era laid down the rules, established the principles, and presented to their society the ideal image of what the Islamic community should live upon when its resources become scarce or the means of livelihood become straitened. And this is what the great scholar al-'Izz ibn Abd al-Salam (2), and Ibn Raf'a (3), and Ibn Jama'a, and other eminent jurists did. Also, the succession of events in this era, the vicissitudes of nights and days, made Muslim scholars observe these events precisely, trace their details with a penetrating eye and a keen mind, so that later generations might take lessons and admonitions. Thus emerged the giant historians, such as al-'Imad Ibn Kathir (1) in his historical encyclopedia (al-Bidaya wa'l-Nihaya), and Ibn Hajar al-'Asqalani (3) in his two attached books (al-Durar al-Kamina fi A'yan al-Mi'a al-Thamina) and the book (Anba' al-Ghumar), and Ibn Khaldun (3) (the pride of the Arabs) in his great encyclopedia called (al-'Ibar) and the Diwan al-Mubtada' wa'l-Khabar fi Tarikh al-'Arab wa'l-'Ajam wa'l-Barbar, and much more. If we went to enumerate the types of knowledge produced by the minds of thinkers in this era, we would be unable to count and enumerate them. It suffices to say in conclusion that the scholarly era of al-Taftazani is an era of giant men and brilliant heroes among thinkers.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive overview of the scholarly environment during the era of al-Taftazani. It begins by mentioning the author's own engagement with deviant ideas, specifically refuting the corrupt thoughts of people like Ibn al-Jawziyya and others. The author then shifts to highlight the positive contributions of jurists during times of hardship, such as wars and famines. These jurists, including al-'Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, Ibn Raf'a, and Ibn Jama'a, codified legal principles and presented an ideal model for Islamic society when resources are scarce. The passage then notes the role of historians, who meticulously documented events to provide lessons for future generations. It mentions prominent historians like Ibn Kathir, Ibn Hajar al-'Asqalani, and Ibn Khaldun, listing some of their major works. The author concludes that the era of al-Taftazani was filled with intellectual giants and that enumerating all their contributions would be impossible. This passage sets the historical and intellectual context for al-Taftazani's own work.

Key Terms
الجوزية
سعد الدين التفتازاني
المعز بن عبد السلام
ابن رفعة
ابن جماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the main contribution of the jurists according to the passage?
A) They fought in wars to restore order.
B) They laid down rules and principles for society.
C) They wrote commentaries on al-Taftazani's works.
D) They rejected all forms of innovation.

Key Takeaway
Even in times of war and famine, jurists like al-'Izz ibn Abd al-Salam and Ibn Jama'a codified principles to sustain Islamic society.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a personal crisis—financial loss, illness, or social upheaval—do your first instincts align with Islamic principles, or do you react with panic and self-reliance? What would it look like for you to 'lay down rules and principles' in your own life during hardship, as the jurists did for their community?
?
The passage contrasts the chaos of thieves with the order brought by jurists. In your own struggles, have you ever compromised your integrity out of desperation? What steps can you take today to ensure that scarcity never pushes you into behavior that contradicts the guidance scholars have preserved for you?
?
The author presents the jurists' work as providing an 'ideal image' for society. Are you actively studying and implementing that ideal in your daily affairs, or have you neglected the principles that sustain faith under pressure? Name one specific principle you will apply this week when you encounter a difficulty.

The Great Historians of the Era
The author highlights the historians who documented events and their major works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of historians in the era of al-Taftazani and the major historical works they produced.

Core Idea
Historians meticulously recorded events to provide lessons for future generations.

The passage continues by emphasizing how the succession of events and changing circumstances led Muslim scholars to document history with precision. This gave rise to monumental historical works by giants like Ibn Kathir, Ibn Hajar, and Ibn Khaldun, who preserved the lessons of their time for posterity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on historians
Read the Arabic passage that describes how historians responded to the events of the era. Notice the phrase 'لتتخذ ذلك الأجيال اللاحقة دروساً وعبراً'.
The purpose of history is clearly stated.
2
Identify the historians and their works
List the three historians mentioned: Ibn Kathir, Ibn Hajar al-'Asqalani, and Ibn Khaldun. Match each with their major work(s) as given in the text.
Note that Ibn Khaldun is called 'مفخرة العرب'.
3
Reflect on the concluding statement
The author concludes that enumerating all knowledge produced in that era is impossible, calling it an era of giants. Consider what makes this period so rich.
This ties back to the theme of scholarly greatness.

Source Text

Al-Jawziyya (1), and Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani and others. I have attacked their degenerate thought and contested their foolish beliefs in error and struck them mortally. And if wars have spread chaos and brought famine upon people, and many members of society have turned into thieves and highway robbers, then the jurists of this era laid down the rules, established the principles, and presented to their society the ideal image of what the Islamic community should live upon when its resources become scarce or the means of livelihood become straitened. Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the main purpose of historical documentation?
A) To entertain readers with stories.
B) To provide lessons and admonitions for later generations.
C) To establish the author's own reputation.
D) To celebrate the achievements of the ruling class.

Historians like Ibn Kathir, Ibn Hajar, and Ibn Khaldun produced monumental works to preserve lessons from the events of their time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about the historians recording events for future lessons, do you take the time to reflect on your own life events and extract lessons from them? What is one recent event in your life that you have not yet learned from, and will you commit to reflecting on it today?
?
The passage states that historians aimed to provide lessons for 'later generations.' Are you actively seeking to benefit from the history of the Muslim ummah, or do you treat it as mere entertainment? Will you read one historical account this week with the intention of finding a personal lesson to apply?
?
The author calls these historians 'giant men' and 'brilliant heroes.' Are you living in a way that will leave a legacy of benefit for those after you? What small, concrete action can you take today to contribute to the preservation of beneficial knowledge or deeds for future generations?

Life of Imam al-Taftazani
beginner
4 min
Prominent historians of the era: Ibn Kathir, Ibn Hajar, Ibn Khaldun
The author lists three major historians and their works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contributions of three major Muslim historians—Ibn Kathir, Ibn Hajar, and Ibn Khaldun—and their key historical works.

Core Idea
Three giants of Islamic historiography: Ibn Kathir, Ibn Hajar, and Ibn Khaldun.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid highlights the era's greatest historians. The author, al-Taftazani, emphasizes how Muslim scholars meticulously recorded events for future generations. Here, he names three towering figures and their monumental works, setting the stage for understanding the richness of Islamic historical scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview.
Pay attention to the names and works mentioned.
2
Identify the historians and their works
List each historian and the work(s) attributed to them: Ibn Kathir – al-Bidaya wa al-Nihaya; Ibn Hajar – al-Durar al-Kamina and Anba' al-Ghumr; Ibn Khaldun – al-`Ibar and Diwan al-Mubtada' wa al-Khabar.
3
Understand the author's purpose
Recognize that al-Taftazani is not just listing names but presenting these historians as exemplars of meticulous scholarship.
The phrase 'giant historians' (المؤرخون العمالقة) indicates their stature.
4
Reflect on the broader context
Consider how these works contributed to preserving Islamic history and how the author's praise underscores their importance.
The passage ends with 'and many others,' showing the vastness of historical writing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جعل علماء المسلمين يرصدون هذه الحوادث بدقة، ويتتبعون جزئياتها، بعين لماحة وفكر متقد، لتتخذ ذلك الأجيال اللاحقة دروساً من وعبراً، فظهر المؤرخون العمالقة، أمثال العماد ابن كثير (۱) في موسوعته التاريخية (البداية والنهاية وابن حجر العسقلاني (٣ ) في كتابيه المحلقين (الدرر الكامنة في (٢) أعيان المائة الثامنة). وكتاب ( أنباء (الغمر وابن خلدون (۳) (مفخرة العرب) في دائرة معارفه الكبرى المسماة العبر) وديوان المبتدأ والخبر في تاريخ العرب والعجم والبربر وغير هذا كثير.
The Muslim scholars made it their practice to observe these events precisely, and to trace their details, with a keen eye and a sharp mind, so that later generations might take from them lessons and admonitions. Then the giant historians emerged, such as `Imad Ibn Kathir (1) in his historical encyclopedia (al-Bidaya wa al-Nihaya), and Ibn Hajar al-`Asqalani (3) in his two accomplished books (al-Durar al-Kamina fi (2) A`yan al-Mi'a al-Thamina) and the book (Anba' al-Ghumr), and Ibn Khaldun (3) (the pride of the Arabs) in his great encyclopedia called al-`Ibar) and the Diwan al-Mubtada' wa al-Khabar on the history of the Arabs, non-Arabs, and Berbers, and many others besides these.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a historical introduction highlighting the leading historians of the era. The author begins by noting how Muslim scholars meticulously recorded events for future generations, resulting in the emergence of 'giant historians'. He then names three: (1) 'Imad al-Din Ibn Kathir, author of the universal history 'al-Bidaya wa al-Nihaya'. (2) Ibn Hajar al-`Asqalani, who wrote two notable works: 'al-Durar al-Kamina' (a biographical dictionary of the 8th century) and 'Anba' al-Ghumr'. (3) Ibn Khaldun, the famous historian and philosopher, who wrote 'al-`Ibar' and 'Diwan al-Mubtada' wa al-Khabar' covering the history of Arabs, Persians, and Berbers. The author concludes that there are many others beyond these. The passage draws attention to the richness of historical scholarship in this period.

Key Terms
العماد ابن كثير
ابن حجر العسقلاني
ابن خلدون
البداية والنهاية
الدرر الكامنة
أنباء الغمر
العبر
ديوان المبتدأ والخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is just listing names without purpose.
Correction: The author is showing the peak of historical scholarship in that era.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among the historians mentioned in this passage?
A) Ibn Kathir
B) Ibn Hajar al-`Asqalani
C) Ibn Khaldun
D) Al-Taftazani

Key Takeaway
Three historians—Ibn Kathir, Ibn Hajar, and Ibn Khaldun—produced foundational works that shaped Islamic historiography.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to write a book about your own life for future generations, what lessons would you want them to learn from your mistakes and successes? Are you living with that legacy in mind?
?
The historians took great care in observing and recording events precisely. How precisely do you observe your own inner state and the events around you? What steps can you take today to become more mindful of the lessons life is offering you?
?
Ibn Khaldun is called 'the pride of the Arabs' for his monumental work. What are you contributing to your community or faith that will be a source of pride and benefit for future generations? If nothing, what can you start doing today?

Socio-Economic Decline After the Mongol and Crusader Invasions
Describes socio-economic decline after Mongol and Crusader attacks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the devastating socio-economic impact of the Mongol and Crusader invasions on the Islamic world in the 7th century AH.

Core Idea
Invasions turned thriving lands into scenes of famine and despair.

This lesson sets the stage for understanding the context in which later scholars worked. The author describes how the fall of Baghdad and subsequent Crusader raids destroyed the economy and caused immense suffering. This background is essential for appreciating the resilience of Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Notice the author's contrast between natural wealth and destruction.
Pay attention to the shift in tone after 'ولكن'.
2
Identify the two main causes
The passage points to the Mongol sack of Baghdad in 656 AH and subsequent Crusader raids. Note how each contributed to economic ruin.
3
Examine the vivid imagery
The author uses powerful similes: invaders as 'predatory wolves' and people preferring death at home. Consider why he chooses these images.
This shows the psychological terror beyond material loss.
4
Analyze Ibn Kathir's quote
The quote describes a specific famine in 718 AH. Note the progression: drought, famine, price rise, eating inanimate objects.
This grounds the general decline in a concrete event.
5
Reflect on the context for scholarship
Ask yourself: How would such hardship affect the production of knowledge? This passage explains why the intellectual efforts of scholars were remarkable.
This connects the lesson to the broader theme of resilience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحالة الاجتماعية لا أحد ينكر أن موارد العالم الإسلامي كثيرة ومتنوعة، وأرضه خصبة ومنتجة، وأهله يميلون إلى العمل وإلى السعي في الأرض، والضرب في فجاجها، ولكن ما حل في القرن السابع الهجري من سقوط الخلافة الإسلامية في بغداد عام ٦٥٦ هـ على يد المغول والتتار، ثم ما حاولته الصليبية في الغرب من انتهاز ضعف المسلمين وشن الغارات عليهم الواحدة بعد الأخرى (١) ، قد أضعف موارد البلاد وأنهك اقتصادياتها، فمزارعها وحدائقها وبساتينها نهباً مباحاً للجيوش المغيرة وكلأً سهلاً أمام شراسة الهجمات، من قوم لا يؤمنون بعقيدة، ولا يهتدون بوحي، الأمر الذي جعلهم يتسلطون على الأهالي الآمنين، تسلط الذئاب المفترسة للقطيع الوديع، والذي جعل الكثير من أفراد الشعب تفضل الموت جوعاً وعطشاً في منازلهم، على الموت في مزارعهم أو حوانيتهم بيد المغيرين المتسلطين . ويصور لنا العماد ابن كثير ما حدث في سنة ٧١٨ هـ فيقول : قل المطر في بلاد الجزيرة والموصل، فحصل الجدب والقحط، وارتفعت الأسعار وعدمت الأقـوات بحيث أكلوا كل ما وجدوا من الجمادات
The Social Condition: No one denies that the resources of the Islamic world are plentiful and diverse, its land fertile and productive, and its people inclined to work and to strive on earth and to traverse its valleys. But what happened in the seventh century AH – the fall of the Islamic caliphate in Baghdad in 656 AH at the hands of the Mongols and Tatars, and then what the Crusaders in the West attempted by exploiting the weakness of the Muslims and launching raids against them one after another (1) – weakened the resources of the countries and exhausted their economies. Their farms, gardens, and orchards became plunder open to the invading armies and easy grazing before the ferocity of attacks by a people who believe in no creed and are guided by no revelation. This caused them to dominate the peaceful inhabitants like predatory wolves dominating a gentle flock, and caused many common people to prefer death by hunger and thirst in their homes over death in their farms or shops at the hands of the attacking invaders. And `Imad Ibn Kathir describes for us what happened in 718 AH, saying: 'Rain decreased in the lands of al-Jazira and Mosul, so drought and famine occurred, prices rose, and foodstuffs disappeared, so that they ate everything they found of inanimate objects.'
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage provides a historical background on social conditions. The author begins by acknowledging the natural wealth and industriousness of the Muslim world. He then identifies two destructive factors: (1) the Mongol sack of Baghdad in 656 AH ending the caliphate, and (2) subsequent Crusader raids from the West exploiting Muslim weakness. These invasions depleted resources and ruined economies. The author uses vivid imagery to describe the devastation: farms and gardens became 'plunder' and 'easy grazing' for invaders who were 'people who believe in no creed' – highlighting their lack of religious restraint. The result was that the peaceful population was terrorized, with many preferring to die at home rather than face the invaders. The author then quotes Ibn Kathir's account of a specific famine in 718 AH in al-Jazira and Mosul, where drought led to famine and people resorted to eating inanimate objects. This passage shows the concrete historical suffering that contextualizes the scholarly efforts mentioned earlier.

سقوط الخلافة الإسلامية
المغول والتتار
الصليبية
الجدب والقحط
العماد ابن كثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is just a dry historical aside with no relevance to understanding the intellectual context.
Correction: The author is setting the emotional and material context for the intellectual activity of the era. The hardships described explain the resilience and urgency of scholarly efforts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what event in the 7th century AH weakened the resources of the Islamic world?
A) The Crusader conquest of Jerusalem
B) The fall of Baghdad to the Mongols
C) The rise of the Ottoman Empire
D) The spread of the plague

Key Takeaway
The Mongol and Crusader invasions caused economic collapse and human suffering that framed the context for Islamic scholarship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that people preferred to die at home rather than face the invaders, what does that reveal about the depth of their fear? Have you ever felt that kind of desperation, and where would you turn if your sense of safety was completely shattered?
?
The author contrasts the invaders—'people who believe in no creed and are guided by no revelation'—with the believing community. In your own life, how does your belief in Allah and His revelation actually guide your actions when you face difficulty or temptation? Are you truly living as if revelation is your guide?
?
Ibn Kathir's description of people eating inanimate objects due to famine is a stark picture of utter helplessness. If you were reduced to that state, would your trust in Allah's decree remain firm? What in your life today could be stripped away to test the reality of your faith?

Understanding the Biography of al-Taftazani
Lists topics of al-Taftazani's biography

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key areas covered in a traditional Islamic scholarly biography, as exemplified by the biography of Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani.

Core Idea
A scholar's biography is a window into the transmission of knowledge.

Before diving into the life of al-Taftazani, this lesson acquaints you with the standard topics that Islamic biographical works typically cover. The author lists the major aspects of al-Taftazani's life and scholarship, giving you a roadmap for the biography that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the list of topics.
2
Identify each topic
Identify each of the six topics listed: name, birth, upbringing, education, teachers, works.
These are the standard components of a scholarly biography.
3
Reflect on purpose
Think about why each topic is important for understanding a scholar's legacy. For example, 'teachers' shows the chain of knowledge transmission.

It outlines the topics that will be covered: his full name and lineage, his birth, his upbringing, his intellectual development and academic formation, his teachers and professors, and his works and writings. The 'VW' at the end is likely a page number. This heading sets the expectation for the subsequent biographical material.

A) His name and lineage
B) His death

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own intellectual formation, what are you actively doing to prepare yourself scientifically, as al-Taftazani's biography highlights his 'intellectual preparation and scientific formation'? Have you treated your learning as a structured process, or have you been passive? Name one concrete step you will take this week to be more deliberate about your intellectual growth.
?
Al-Taftazani's biography includes his teachers. Who are the teachers—living or through books—that you are learning from? Have you sought out qualified scholars to guide you, or are you relying on scattered sources without a consistent mentor? Commit to identifying one teacher or source you will follow systematically from today.
?
The biography also lists al-Taftazani's works and writings. What are you producing for the sake of knowledge? Even a small written reflection or teaching a single concept counts. If you were to leave a legacy of knowledge, what would it be? Write down one idea you can develop into a shareable piece of knowledge this month.

The Correct Name of al-Taftazani
The author discusses the consensus on al-Taftazani's name and refutes Ibn Hajar's differing claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scholarly consensus affirms al-Taftazani's name as Mas'ud and why Ibn Hajar's variant is rejected as an error.

Core Idea
Consensus outweighs a single dissenting voice, even from a great scholar.

This lesson examines a biographical dispute over the name of the renowned scholar al-Taftazani. While the vast majority of biographers agree he is Mas'ud ibn 'Umar, the famous hadith scholar Ibn Hajar al-'Asqalani claimed his name was Mahmud. The author of our text firmly sides with the consensus and offers an explanation for the discrepancy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for its flow, then the English translation. Notice the author's confident tone as he lists authorities.
The passage includes footnotes; you can ignore them for now.
2
Identify the consensus view
Note the names of the scholars who agree on 'Mas'ud': al-Suyuti, the author of Shadharat al-Dhahab, Tashkubri Zada, and al-Baghdadi. The author calls this the 'vast majority'.
3
Understand Ibn Hajar's solitary claim
Ibn Hajar alone says the name is 'Mahmud'. The author expresses strong disagreement and wonders how such a careful scholar made this error.
The author does not accuse Ibn Hajar of bad faith but suggests scribal errors.
4
Reflect on the author's explanation
The author attributes the mistake to tashif (scribal distortions) and even possible deliberate defamation by copyists. Ask yourself: why does the author reject Ibn Hajar's version?
This shows the principle of preferring widespread testimony over a single report.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسمه ونسبه
تتفق الكثرة الكثيرة من أصحاب كتب التراجم على أنه هو: مسعود بن عمر ابن عبد الله التفتازاني . ذكر ذلك الإمام السيوطي في كتابه (بغية الوعاة) (۱) فهو عنده مسعود بن عمر بن عبد الله التفتازاني . وكذلك صاحب (شذرات الذهب) (۲) وطاشكبري زاده في كتابه مفتاح السعادة) (۳) والإمام البغدادي في (هدية العارفين) (٤) .
ويكاد ينفرد العلامة ابن حجر في كتابه أنباء الغم)(٥) وفي موضع آخر من كتابه (الدرر الكامنة (٦) من أن اسمه هو (محمود) بدلاً من مسعود وهذا مما لا نوافقه عليه ولا ندري كيف وقع العلامة الكبير، المدقق الحصيف، في تحريف اسم أحد الأعلام الذي تزدان المكتبة العربية والإسلامية بزخائر كتبه ومؤلفاته، وإن كنا نرجع ذلك إلى التصحيفات والتحريفات التي يقع فيها كثير من نساخ المخطوطات بالإضافة إلى التحريفات التي يعتمدها بعض الأفراد رغبة في النكاية والتشهير.
(۱) بغية الوعاة ص .۳۹۱
(۲) شذرات الذهب ج ٦ ص ٣١٩ إلى ص ٣٣٢ . (۳) مفتاح السعادة ج ١ ص ١٦٤ - ص ١٦٧ . (٤) هدية العارفين ج ۲ ص ٠٤٢٩ ص ٤٣٠ . (5) أنباء الغمرج ٢ ص ١٤ . (٦) الدرر الكامنة ج ٤ ص ٣٤٠ .
His name and lineage. The vast majority of authors of biographical works agree that he is: Mas'ud ibn 'Umar ibn 'Abd Allah al-Taftazani. Al-Imam al-Suyuti mentioned that in his book (Bughyat al-Wu'at) (1), so according to him he is Mas'ud ibn 'Umar ibn 'Abd Allah al-Taftazani. Likewise the author of (Shadharat al-Dhahab) (2) and Tashkubri Zada in his book (Miftah al-Sa'ada) (3) and al-Imam al-Baghdadi in (Hadiyat al-'Arifin) (4). And al-'Allama Ibn Hajar is almost alone in his book (Inba' al-Ghumr) (5) and in another place in his book (al-Durar al-Kamina) (6) in that his name is (Mahmud) instead of Mas'ud. And this is something we do not agree with him on, and we do not know how the great, precise, and discerning scholar fell into distorting the name of one of the eminent figures whose books and works adorn the Arabic and Islamic library, though we attribute that to the scribal errors and distortions that many copyists of manuscripts fall into, in addition to the distortions that some individuals adopt out of a desire for spite and defamation. (1) Bughyat al-Wu'at, p. 391. (2) Shadharat al-Dhahab, vol. 6, pp. 319-332. (3) Miftah al-Sa'ada, vol. 1, pp. 164-167. (4) Hadiyat al-'Arifin, vol. 2, pp. 429-430. (5) Inba' al-Ghumr, vol. 2, p. 14. (6) al-Durar al-Kamina, vol. 4, p. 340.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a biographical note that establishes the author's name. It begins by stating that the overwhelming consensus among biographical sources is that the author is Mas'ud ibn 'Umar ibn 'Abd Allah al-Taftazani. The author then cites specific authorities: al-Suyuti in Bughyat al-Wu'at, the author of Shadharat al-Dhahab, Tashkubri Zada, and al-Baghdadi. After establishing this consensus, the passage notes that Ibn Hajar al-'Asqalani is virtually alone in claiming that the name is Mahmud instead of Mas'ud. The author expresses disagreement and skepticism about Ibn Hajar's claim, wondering how such a meticulous scholar could have made this mistake. The passage concludes by attributing the discrepancy to scribal errors (tashif) and distortions introduced by copyists, or even deliberate falsification by some individuals for spite and defamation. Importantly, the author does not accept Ibn Hajar's variant as authoritative, reinforcing the reliability of the majority view.

Key Terms
التفتازاني
مسعود
ابن حجر
تصحيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ibn Hajar's solitary report carries equal weight to the consensus of other biographers.
Correction: The passage shows the author rejects Ibn Hajar's claim as an error, likely due to scribal mistakes, emphasizing that consensus is the standard.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct name of al-Taftazani?
A) Mas'ud ibn 'Umar ibn 'Abd Allah
B) Mahmud ibn 'Umar ibn 'Abd Allah
C) Both names are equally attested
D) The text does not specify

Key Takeaway
The overwhelming consensus of biographical sources confirms al-Taftazani's name as Mas'ud, and Ibn Hajar's solitary variant is rejected as a scribal error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a single person’s opinion that contradicts the overwhelming majority of scholars on a matter of religion, do you automatically assume both opinions are equally valid? How does this lesson challenge that assumption in your own heart?
?
Are you quick to accept a sensational claim from a respected authority, or do you weigh it against the consensus? Give yourself a real example from your own life where you might have followed a lone opinion without checking the majority.
?
The author attributes Ibn Hajar’s error partly to spite and defamation. Have you ever accepted or spread a story about a scholar or institution because it satisfied your own biases, without verifying it against the consensus? What would it cost you to change that habit?

Taftazani's Correct Name: Mas'ud or Mahmud?
Defines al-Taftazani's name and refutes a variant claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the correct name of al-Taftazani is Mas'ud and how a conflicting claim arose.

Core Idea
The majority of biographers agree: al-Taftazani's name is Mas'ud, not Mahmud.

This lesson addresses a common biographical question: what is the correct name of the famous scholar al-Taftazani? The passage presents the consensus of major biographers and then discusses a rare dissenting opinion, explaining how such discrepancies can occur. It reminds us of the importance of careful scholarship in preserving accurate knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about details. Then read the English translation. Notice the flow: first the consensus, then the exception, then the author's commentary.
Look out for the names of the biographers mentioned.
2
Identify the two views
Identify the majority view (Mas'ud) and the minority view (Mahmud). Note who holds each view.
Ibn Hajar is the only one mentioned for the minority view.
3
Understand the author's stance
Notice how the author strongly disagrees with Ibn Hajar and offers possible reasons for the error.
The author attributes the mistake to copyist errors or deliberate distortion.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider why getting a scholar's name right matters in Islamic scholarship.
This case illustrates a broader principle about verifying names and avoiding distortions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسمه ونسبه
تتفق الكثرة الكثيرة من أصحاب كتب التراجم على أنه هو: مسعود بن عمر ابن عبد الله التفتازاني . ذكر ذلك الإمام السيوطي في كتابه (بغية الوعاة) (۱) فهو عنده مسعود بن عمر بن عبد الله التفتازاني . وكذلك صاحب (شذرات الذهب) (۲) وطاشكبري زاده في كتابه مفتاح السعادة) (۳) والإمام البغدادي في (هدية العارفين) (٤) .
ويكاد ينفرد العلامة ابن حجر في كتابه أنباء الغم)(٥) وفي موضع آخر من كتابه (الدرر الكامنة (٦) من أن اسمه هو (محمود) بدلاً من مسعود وهذا مما لا نوافقه عليه ولا ندري كيف وقع العلامة الكبير، المدقق الحصيف، في تحريف اسم أحد الأعلام الذي تزدان المكتبة العربية والإسلامية بزخائر كتبه ومؤلفاته، وإن كنا نرجع ذلك إلى التصحيفات والتحريفات التي يقع فيها كثير من نساخ المخطوطات بالإضافة إلى التحريفات التي يعتمدها بعض الأفراد رغبة في النكاية والتشهير.
(۱) بغية الوعاة ص .۳۹۱
(۲) شذرات الذهب ج ٦ ص ٣١٩ إلى ص ٣٣٢ . (۳) مفتاح السعادة ج ١ ص ١٦٤ - ص ١٦٧ . (٤) هدية العارفين ج ۲ ص ٠٤٢٩ ص ٤٣٠ . (5) أنباء الغمرج ٢ ص ١٤ . (٦) الدرر الكامنة ج ٤ ص ٣٤٠ .
His name and lineage: The vast majority of authors of biographical works agree that he is: Mas'ud ibn 'Umar ibn 'Abd Allah al-Taftazani. Imam al-Suyuti mentioned that in his book (Bughyat al-Wu'ah) (1); according to him, he is Mas'ud ibn 'Umar ibn 'Abd Allah al-Taftazani. Likewise the author of (Shadharat al-Dhahab) (2), Tashkubri Zada in his book (Miftah al-Sa'adah) (3), and Imam al-Baghdadi in (Hadiyat al-'Arifin) (4). And 'Allama Ibn Hajar nearly uniquely in his book (Inba' al-Ghumr) (5) and in another place in his book (al-Durar al-Kaminah) (6) holds that his name is Mahmud instead of Mas'ud, and this is something we do not agree with him on, and we do not know how the great, precise, and judicious scholar fell into distorting the name of one of the eminent figures whose books and works adorn the Arabic and Islamic library, although we attribute that to the miswritings and distortions that many copyists of manuscripts fall into, in addition to distortions adopted by some individuals out of a desire for spite and defamation. (1) Bughyat al-Wu'ah p. 391. (2) Shadharat al-Dhahab vol. 6 pp. 319-332. (3) Miftah al-Sa'adah vol. 1 pp. 164-167. (4) Hadiyat al-'Arifin vol. 2 pp. 429-430. (5) Inba' al-Ghumr vol. 2 p. 14. (6) al-Durar al-Kaminah vol. 4 p. 340.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a biographical definition and refutation. It begins by stating the name of the scholar (al-Taftazani) as Mas'ud ibn 'Umar ibn 'Abd Allah, noting that the vast majority of biographical sources agree on this. It then lists several authorities who confirm this name: al-Suyuti, the author of Shadharat al-Dhahab, Tashkubri Zada, and al-Baghdadi. The author then points out that Ibn Hajar almost alone claims that the name is Mahmud instead of Mas'ud, citing two of his works. The author expresses strong disagreement and astonishment that such a careful scholar could make this error. He suggests that the mistake may be due to copyist errors or deliberate distortions by certain individuals seeking to cause harm. The passage thus defends the correct name and attributes the variant to textual corruption.

Key Terms
تفتازاني
مسعود
محمود
تحريف
تراجم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name of al-Taftazani is Mahmud.
Correction: The correct name is Mas'ud, as affirmed by the vast majority of biographers. The claim of Mahmud is a distortion likely arising from copyist errors or deliberate alteration.

Checkpoint
What name does the author reject for al-Taftazani?
A) Mas'ud
B) Mahmud
C) 'Abd Allah
D) 'Umar

Key Takeaway
The correct name of al-Taftazani is Mas'ud, not Mahmud, as affirmed by the majority of biographers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a scholar you deeply respect says something that contradicts clear evidence, are you willing to humbly disagree? What is the danger of blind following, even of great scholars?
?
The author attributes some distortions to spite and defamation. Have you ever spread unverified information about a scholar? What would it take to stop and check original sources before speaking?
?
The author expresses astonishment that a 'precise and judicious scholar' could err. When you disagree with someone, are you quick to assume you are correct? How can you cultivate intellectual humility?

Life of Imam al-Taftazani
beginner
4 min
Dispute Over al-Taftazani's Birth Year
Records two conflicting dates for al-Taftazani's birth and supports the earlier date.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that despite conflicting reports, the majority of biographical sources support the birth of al-Taftazani in Safar 722 AH.

Core Idea
Al-Taftazani's birth year is 722 AH, confirmed by his tomb inscription.

Biographical works often disagree on dates. This passage examines two reported birth years for al-Taftazani and presents strong evidence for the earlier one, including an inscription on his grave. Let's explore the sources that confirm this date.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Notice that the passage begins by stating that differences in birth and death dates are common in biographical literature. This sets up the context for the dispute over al-Taftazani's birth year.
2
Examine the primary evidence
Read about the report from Tashkubri Zada, who transmitted from Fath Allah al-Shirwani, a student of al-Taftazani. Al-Shirwani saw the birth year inscribed on al-Taftazani's grave in Sarakhs.
This is the strongest piece of evidence.
3
Identify additional support
Note that the same date was also found written on old copies of al-Mutawwal, and that later scholars like al-Baha'i, al-Baghdadi, al-Shawkani, and others confirm it.
The consensus reinforces the 722 AH date.

Source Text

أنه ولد في صفر سنة ٧٢٢ هـ وهذا التاريخ يؤكده طاشكبري زاده لأنه نقله عن فتح الله الشرواني تلميذ التفتازاني والذي ذكر في أوائل شرحه لكتاب أستاذه (الإرشاد) أنه زار مرقده بسرخس فوجد مكتوباً عليه ولد عليه الرحمة والرضوان في صفر سنة ۷۲۲ هـ ) ويؤيد هذا التاريخ أيضاً الذي ذكره فتح الله الشرواني أنه وجد مكتوباً على ظهر بعض نسخ المطول، وينقل هذا عن صاحب (روضات الجنات) (۲) حيث يقول إن هذا التاريخ وجدته على بعض نسخ المطول القديمة، ويوافقه أيضاً الشيخ البهائي فيقول :

His birth: The peruser of biographical works finds two dates for the birth of al-Sa'd, and the difference in the dates of birth and death of eminent figures is nearly a general phenomenon that no book of biographies is free of. For example, the subject of the translation before us: Tashkubri Zada mentions about him (1) that he was born in Safar of the year 722 AH, and this date is confirmed by Tashkubri Zada because he transmitted it from Fath Allah al-Shirwani, the student of al-Taftazani, who mentioned in the beginning of his commentary on his teacher's book (al-Irshad) that he visited his grave in Sarakhs and found written on it: 'Born, may mercy and pleasure be upon him, in Safar of the year 722 AH.' This date is also supported by what Fath Allah al-Shirwani mentioned, that he found it written on the back of some copies of al-Mutawwal, and this is transmitted from the author of (Rawdat al-Jannat) (2) who says: 'I found this date on some old copies of al-Mutawwal.' Also, Shaykh al-Baha'i agrees with him, saying: 'The 'Allama al-Taftazani was born in Safar of the year 722 AH and died in the year 792 AH, so his age was 70 years.' This date is confirmed by Imam al-Baghdadi in (Hadiyat al-'Arifin) (3), al-Shawkani in (al-Badr al-Tali') (4), al-Kanawi in (Kata'ib A'lam al-Akhyar) (5), the Encyclopedia of Islam (6), and Armenius Vambery in (History of Bukhara) (7). (1) Miftah al-Sa'adah vol. 1 p. 304. (2) Rawdat al-Jannat p. 309. (3) Hadiyat al-'Arifin vol. 2 p. 429. (4) al-Badr al-Tali' vol. 2 p. 303. (5) Kata'ib A'lam al-Akhyar p. 434. (6) Encyclopedia of Islam vol. 9 p. 402. (7) History of Bukhara p. 246.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a biographical discussion of al-Taftazani's birth date. It begins by noting that discrepancies in birth and death dates are common in biographical works. The author then presents two reported dates. The first and more strongly supported date is Safar 722 AH, transmitted by Tashkubri Zada from Fath Allah al-Shirwani, al-Taftazani's student, who saw it inscribed on his tomb in Sarakhs. Additional support comes from a note on old copies of al-Mutawwal, cited by the author of Rawdat al-Jannat. Shaykh al-Baha'i also affirms this date, stating that al-Taftazani lived 70 years (722-792 AH). The passage then lists many later authorities who confirm this date: al-Baghdadi, al-Shawkani, al-Kanawi, the Encyclopedia of Islam, and Vambery. The passage thus argues for the reliability of the 722 AH birth year.

Resolving the Birth Year of al-Taftazani
The author reviews two conflicting reports on al-Taftazani's birth year and argues for 722 AH.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two conflicting reports on al-Taftazani's birth year and the evidence that leads the author to prefer 722 AH.

Core Idea
Disagreement over birth dates is common; al-Taftazani's own works confirm he was born in 722 AH.

Biographical sources often disagree on dates of birth and death. For the great scholar Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, there are two main views: 712 AH and 722 AH. This lesson examines the evidence for each and shows why the author favors the later date, using al-Taftazani's own early writings as the key clue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two views
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two different birth years mentioned (722 AH and 712 AH) and the scholars who support each view.
Pay attention to the names: Tashkubri Zadah, Fathallah al-Shirwani, Ibn Hajar, etc.
2
Analyze the evidence for each view
Identify the specific evidence cited for the 722 AH view (grave inscription, notes on al-Mutawwal, consensus of later scholars) and for the 712 AH view (Ibn al-Jazari's handwriting, transmitted by Ibn Hajar).
The author highlights that the 722 view has multiple independent sources.
3
Understand the author's preference and reasoning
Read the section where the author states his preference for 722 AH. Note the internal evidence: al-Taftazani's first book at age 16 in 738 AH, and his starting al-Mutawwal at age 20 in 742 AH, both consistent with a 722 birth.
This is the key argument: the author uses al-Taftazani's own scholarly timeline to confirm the birth year.
